<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="cabaa55444994009aefb0662475b20db">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ce7bf85d-5397-4b25-8752-a46317c62be3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Qian/Yitai">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Xiong/Shenglin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Shenglin Xiong<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Shenglin Xiong</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:chexsl@sdu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>chexsl@sdu.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Key Laboratory of the Colloid and Interface Chemistry, Ministry of Education and School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Shandong University, Jinan, 250100 P. R. China</p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:linyue@ustc.edu.cn"><span>linyue@ustc.edu.cn</span></a>, <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:chexsl@sdu.edu.cn"><span>chexsl@sdu.edu.cn</span></a><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Xiong/Shenglin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d227705687" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered promising next-generation energy storage devices. However, a lack of appropriate high-performance anode materials has prevented further improvements. Here, a hierarchical porous hybrid nanosheet composed of interconnected uniform TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and nitrogen-doped graphene layer networks (TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs) that are synthesized using dual-functional C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanosheets as both the self-sacrificing template and hybrid carbon source is reported. These HPHNSs deliver high reversible capacities of 146 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C for 8000 cycles, 129 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C for 20 000 cycles, and 116 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C for 10 000 cycles, as well as an ultrahigh rate capability up to 60 C with a capacity of 101 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. These results demonstrate the longest cyclabilities and best rate capability ever reported for TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials for SIBs. The unprecedented sodium storage performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs is due to their unique composition and hierarchical porous 2D structure.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201705788-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Currently, environmental issues, such as global warming and air pollution, have resulted in a relentless pursuit of renewable energy technologies, such as wind and solar power. However, because these natural energy sources are intermittent, variable, and heavily dependent on the weather, season, and location, integrating storage solutions into the renewable energy strategy is a high priority for sustainable energy grids.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0001_R_d227705608e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></span> Among various storage solutions, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as the most competitive electrochemical technology able to satisfy renewable energy demands because of their low cost and the natural abundance of sodium.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0002_R_d227705608e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0003_R_d227705608e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Chem.">3</a></span> One pivotal challenge is to design optimal SIB anodes that can reversibly accommodate the large Na ions.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0004_R_d227705608e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Acc. Chem. Res.">4</a></span> In this respect, insertion-type anodes are thought to be the most promising since these materials can reversibly intercalate foreign Na ions while maintaining their structural integrity. Representative intercalation anodes include disordered carbons,<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0005_R_d227705608e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0007_R_d227705608e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></span> Ti-based oxides (TiO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, and Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>),<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0008_R_d227705608e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0010_R_d227705608e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></span> and niobium pentoxide.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0011_R_d227705608e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></span> Since Ti-based oxide materials were proposed as anode materials for SIBs in 2011,<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0012_R_d227705608e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">12</a></span> great efforts have been devoted to developing different strategies to prepare polymorphic TiO<sub>2</sub>-based structures.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0013_R_d227705608e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Lett.">13</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0020_R_d227705608e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span> For example, Myung and co-workers synthesized carbon-sheathed anatase-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods to improve the rate capability.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0013_R_d227705608e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Lett.">13</a></span> Huang and co-workers reported the fabrication of TiO<sub>2</sub>-B/graphene hybrids, which had enhanced sodium storage properties.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0014_R_d227705608e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></span> Ji and co-workers reported the synthesis of graphene-wrapped rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> spheres for high-performance sodium storage.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0015_R_d227705608e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></span> Very recently, Le et al. confirmed the high capacitive contribution of fast sodium storage in anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>-graphene composites.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0016_R_d227705608e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano">16</a></span> However, the capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes, especially at higher rates, require further improvements to meet the requirements of electric vehicles with high energy consumption. Additionally, in intercalation-based anode materials, the tradeoff between the real electrochemical performance and structural stability should be fully considered. Therefore, exploring an effective strategy to engineer the structure, morphology, and composition of the battery materials to improve their performance while maintaining the structural integrity of the electrodes is highly desirable but challenging.</p>
         
         <p>As mentioned above, the structure of the carbon-based hybrids is generally one of two typical models: (1) active ­species growing on the surface of graphene or (2) coating a ­material with 3D carbon.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0013_R_d227705608e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Lett.">13</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0016_R_d227705608e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano">16</a></span> However, growth on the surface of ­graphene does not result in a solid contact between the active materials and ­graphene, which can reduce the electrical conductivity. In ­contrast, in the second model, the active materials are not exposed to the electrolyte, which will inevitably result in a lower availability of the host material, a longer transfer distance for the electrons and ions, and a weakened active material performance. Recently, a class of newly emerging 2D carbon nitride (C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>) materials has attracted increasing research interest.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0021_R_d227705608e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0022_R_d227705608e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></span> Despite considerable effort devoted to the preparation of C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>-based composite materials<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0023_R_d227705608e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0024_R_d227705608e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a></span> and derivative materials templated from C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0025_R_d227705608e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0029_R_d227705608e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></span> to the best of our knowledge, the dual-­functional properties of C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> 2D nanosheets are currently unknown.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, for the first time, we report the synthesis of novel nitrogen-doped few-layered graphene-wrapped TiO<sub>2</sub> ­hierarchical porous hybrid nanosheets (denoted TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs) using dual-functional C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> sheets as both the sacrificial template and hybrid carbon source. In the as-synthesized structure, interconnected uniform TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are ­confined in the continuous ultrathin graphitic carbon network (&lt;3 layers) and preferentially assemble into hierarchical porous 2D nanosheets. Compared with previous reports on carbon-based composite electrodes, our 2D hybrid electrode possesses the following unusual advantages: (1) the low content (&lt;5 wt%) of graphitic carbon tightly coating the surface of the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles offers continuous 2D electrical conduction and greatly increases the active material availability; (2) the hierarchical porous 2D nanosheets permit easy electrolyte permeation and mitigate the volume change upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction; (3) the formation of the carbon coating restricts the size of the uniform TiO<sub>2</sub> ­nanoparticles, improving the structural stability. ­First-­principle calculations confirmed that the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid could enhance the charge transfer reaction kinetics, particularly at the interface. The kinetics analysis revealed that the capacitance and diffusion mechanisms were responsible for the Na<sup>+</sup> storage, and the former mechanism was dominant. Therefore, the resultant TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs have an excellent sodium storage performance with a high specific capacity, ultrafast pseudocapacitive sodium storage capability, and ultralong cycling stability. ­Specifically, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs delivered a high reversible capacity of 185 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and decent initial coulombic efficiency (CE) of ≈49% at a current density of 0.5 C (1 C = 335 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). ­Dramatically, the HPHNSs ­exhibited excellent cycling stability at 5 C for over 8000 cycles with a reversible capacity of 146 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, at 10 C for over ­20 000 cycles with a reversible capacity of 129 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and at 20 C for over 10 000 cycles with a reversible capacity of 116 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid also showed an unprecedented high rate capability up to 60 C (20.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) with a reversible capacity up to 101 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. These three extremes confirm the advantages of the unique 2D hierarchical porous hybrid nanostructure, which will be further discussed in detail.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0001">1</a> schematically illustrates the preparation of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs. Specifically, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> precursor was first synthesized via a typical pyrolysis method according to a previously reported procedure (Figure S1, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0026_R_d227705608e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></span> Briefly, tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) was added into a g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>-ethanol-ammonia suspension at room temperature under magnetic stirring. Then, the above suspension was maintained at 45 °C for 20 h in a round-bottom flask to obtain flake-like g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S2, Supporting Information). After calcination at 600 °C for 2 h under vacuum, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs were harvested (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0001">1</a>(i); see the Experimental Section for the preparation details).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201705788-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/064b95da-a6cd-43b3-bf39-0b85818c0fc8/adma201705788-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/064b95da-a6cd-43b3-bf39-0b85818c0fc8/adma201705788-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/42c2f898-967f-4949-815a-b0aa41c7d56a/adma201705788-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/064b95da-a6cd-43b3-bf39-0b85818c0fc8/adma201705788-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201705788-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201705788" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the preparation of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs, (i) reflux synthesis using g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as the substrates for the preparation of TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs at 600 °C under vacuum. (ii) Synthesis at 600 °C in air for the formation of m-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets composed of nonuniform nanoparticles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The as-obtained TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs are first examined by X-ray diffraction (XRD) to confirm its crystallorganic structure. As shown in Figure S3 (Supporting Information), all the diffraction peaks can be indexed to the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 21-1272, space group I41/amd (141)). No g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> peaks were detected, indicating the complete decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> at high temperatures. For comparison, the corresponding precursor was calcined in the ambient air (see Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0001">1</a>(ii)) and denoted m-TiO<sub>2</sub> (m means mixed); the XRD peaks can be indexed to the anatase and rutile phases due to the phase inversion of TiO<sub>2</sub> at 600 °C under air (Figure S4, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0030_R_d227705608e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem.">30</a></span></p>
         
         <p>The morphology and detailed microstructure of the as-­synthesized TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs were investigated using field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The panoramic view for the solid sample (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>A) reveals a uniform, sheet-like network structure with a lateral size of more than 1 µm. A single nanosheet is shown in the magnified FESEM image in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>B. The nanosheet is porous and composed of numerous primary particles with a size of ≈10 nm. The corresponding high-magnification FESEM images in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>C and Figure S5 (Supporting Information) confirm that the thickness of the monolayer nanosheet was ≈10–15 nm. A standing nanosheet, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>C, clearly demonstrates the double or triple-layered structure (indicated by white and red arrows) and the porous structure. Atomic force microscopy (AFM) was performed to further evaluate the thickness of the as-synthesized nanosheets, as shown in Figure S6 (Supporting Information). The measured heights of the nanosheet regions were ≈10, 20, and 30 nm, corresponding to 1, 2, and 3 layers, respectively, which is consistent with the FESEM observations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201705788-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eddb1e99-c91a-450a-bf25-80346863fe87/adma201705788-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eddb1e99-c91a-450a-bf25-80346863fe87/adma201705788-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52083efe-dbff-48eb-9b77-d8a3051155de/adma201705788-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eddb1e99-c91a-450a-bf25-80346863fe87/adma201705788-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201705788-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201705788" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structure of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid. A–C) FESEM images. D,E) TEM images. The inset in (E) shows the corresponding SAED pattern. F) HRTEM image of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid showing the distinguishable microstructures, lattice fringes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> and ­few-layered ­graphene coated on the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals (as shown by the red arrows and dashed lines). G–I) HAADF-STEM images of a typical TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG nanosheet. HAADF image and the corresponding EELS maps of K) Ti, L) O, M) N, and N) C, and O) the merged image.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The TEM images (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>D and Figure S7A, Supporting Information) reveal that the porous structures of these nanosheets with pore sizes mainly less than 20 nm are composed of interconnected and uniform nanocrystals with diameters of ≈10 nm. A magnified TEM image is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>E. The ring-like mode in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (inset of Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>E) demonstrates the presence of polycrystalline TiO<sub>2</sub>, which can be indexed as anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 21-1272) and agrees with the XRD analysis (Figure S3, Supporting Information). The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>F and Figure S7B–D, Supporting Information) show that the interconnected TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are coated with a continuous carbon layer of layered graphene (1–3 layers thick) to form a network structure. The few-layered graphene coating on the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals is indicated by red arrows and dashed lines in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>F. The lattice fringes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> can be observed. A TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystal was imaged along the [11–1] zone axis, and the results agree with the XRD analysis. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) technique was adopted to study the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs. Low-magnification HAADF images are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>G,H and show porous structures, which coincide with the TEM images. An atomic-resolution HAADF image is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>I. A TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystal along the [010] zone axis was imaged using the HAADF technique. The atomic structure agrees with the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> model shown in the inset in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>I. The few-layered graphene coated on the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals can be indistinctly seen in the HAADF image. To confirm the few-layered graphene coating, the elemental distribution of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs was further investigated via electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) maps. In Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0002">2</a>J–O, the EELS maps show the elemental distribution of Ti (green), O (cyan), N (yellow), and C (red) in the nanosheet and clearly reveal the graphene-wrapped structure. This finding is consistent with the HRTEM observations and suggests that the intimate contact between the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and N-doped ultrathin carbon network layer in the nanosheets provides effective electron transportation in this 2D hybrid composite. The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid in air indicated a weight loss of ≈4.9 wt% between 300 and 500 °C, which corresponds to the loss of carbon in the hybrid material (Figure S8, Supporting Information). Hence, the NFG content in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG sample was ≈4.9 wt%. Further materials characterization with Raman spectroscopy and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) can be found in the Supporting Information (Figures S9–S12, Supporting Information). As can be seen from Figure S12D (Supporting Information), the high-­resolution N 1s XPS spectrum indicates the existence of pyridinic N (N-6, 398.5 eV), pyrrolic N (N-5, 400.1 eV), and ­quaternary N (N-Q, 401.2 eV) in the hybrid nanosheets and confirms that the N doping concentration is ≈5.2% in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs. N-6 and N-5, which are functional groups in the electrochemically active sites, can increase the surface-induced capacitive processes involving sodium ions<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0031_R_d227705608e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0032_R_d227705608e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></span> and improve the fast diffusion of the reactive sodium ions.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0010_R_d227705608e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></span> N-Q is located inside the graphitic carbon plane and bonds with three sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms, which benefits the conductivity of the ­graphitic carbon because of the considerable change in the electron–donor feature.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0033_R_d227705608e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0034_R_d227705608e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></span> Moreover, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs have a high specific Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 101 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> with pore sizes mostly less than 20 nm (Figure S13, Supporting Information). Some of the pores were in the 20–45 and 50–80 nm size ranges, indicating the coexistence of coarser mesoporous and macroporous features in the nanosheets. The selection of an appropriate atmosphere for the calcination treatment plays a crucial role in the generation of the unique hierarchical porous structures. When air was used in the calcination process instead of a vacuum, TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets composed only of uneven nanoparticles with sizes of ≈10–100 nm were obtained without a carbon coating (Figure S14, Supporting Information). This phenomenon demonstrates that the formation of coated carbon layers can efficiently restrict the size of the TiO<sub>2</sub> and result in small, uniform particles. Furthermore, m-TiO<sub>2</sub> had a relatively low surface area of 56 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> without an abundant pore size distribution (Figure S15, Supporting Information). The hierarchically porous structures of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid nanosheets aid the diffusion of Na ions and can improve the contact between the electrode and electrolyte during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reaction to enhance the electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>The successful synthesis of 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs as SIB anodes is evident from the excellent electrochemical behaviors (as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>, Figures S16 and S17, Supporting Information). Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>A shows the first five cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid electrode between 0.01 and 3.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Clearly, the first CV curve is different from those of the subsequent cycles, especially the discharge portion of the curve, which coincides with the reported literature.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0037_R_d227705608e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Power Sources">37</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0038_R_d227705608e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">38</a></span> In the first cycle, the broad reduction peak at 0.97 V in the cathodic process can be assigned to the reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>3+</sup> and the formation of a solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0039_R_d227705608e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></span> One broad oxidation peak is observed at 0.83 V in the following anodic scan and corresponds to the oxidation of Ti<sup>3+</sup> to Ti<sup>4+</sup>. In the following cycles, the reduction peak gradually moves to a potential of 0.63 V. Importantly, after the first circle, the CV curves overlap well, indicating the good stability and cyclability of the material for the insertion and extraction of sodium ions. Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>B shows the typical discharge/charge voltage profiles of TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs at 0.5 C (1 C = 335 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). There is one plateau between 0.6 and 0.8 V for all the discharge curves. This voltage platform indicates that the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs are suitable for SIBs rather than lithium-ion batteries, which have a voltage platform above 1 V. The plateau of the first discharge curve is higher than that of the others, which may be related to the irreversible reaction of TiO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> and agrees with the CV results. Interestingly, the hybrid displays a moderate first CE of ≈49%, which could be due to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O and the SEI as well as the irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0040_R_d227705608e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0041_R_d227705608e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></span> Notably, an obvious change in both the charge and discharge profiles with a voltage plateau at ≈0.6–0.8 V is not observed, even after 800 cycles, which shows that the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs are stable during prolonged cycling.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201705788-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca37638b-5108-4d66-a1f5-f82c4c099e0e/adma201705788-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca37638b-5108-4d66-a1f5-f82c4c099e0e/adma201705788-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/36caa12e-2a8b-4a6b-b1b6-821d5dc8949f/adma201705788-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca37638b-5108-4d66-a1f5-f82c4c099e0e/adma201705788-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201705788-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201705788" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode. A) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. B) Charge–discharge profiles for selected cycles at a rate of 0.5 C. C,D) Cycling performance at rates of 0.5 C and (D) 2 C. E) Rate cycling behavior. F) Comparison of the rate capability of TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG with that of other TiO<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes recently reported. The capacities were normalized to the same C rate (1 C = 335 mA g<sup>−1</sup>).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To explore the superiority of the unique 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs as anode materials for SIBs, we measured the cycle performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode at 0.5 and 2 C. For comparison, m-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were also tested under the same conditions. All the results are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>C,D. Obviously, the 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode had a much higher reversible capacity than that of the m-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. The electrode had a high reversible capacity of 185 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C after 800 cycles and 162 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C after 2200 cycles, which indicates a facile, repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction related to the efficient transport of ions and electrons in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>As expected, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG hybrid electrode exhibited an extremely durable high rate capability, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>E. As the current density increased from 0.25 to 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30, and 40 C, the reversible capacity slightly decreased from 205 to 190, 170, 157, 140, 129, 120, 116, and 110 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Unexpectedly, when the rates further increased to 50 and 60 C, the reversible capacities were still ≈107 and 101 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, after the high rate measurement, the current density returned to 5 C, and a reversible capacity of 140 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was recovered and maintained for more than 3000 cycles without any losses and with a CE of almost 100% (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>E and Figure S16, Supporting Information). The results suggest that the structure of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs is exceedingly stable, even under an ultrahigh rate and ultralong cycling, which is important for industrial applications. In contrast, the m-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode had a considerably lower capacity (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>E), which further demonstrates the advantages of using the 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs for sodium storage. To further verify the durability of our hybrid anode at higher rates, the cycling performance was further tested at 5, 10, and 20 C (Figure S17, Supporting Information). Three cycles were first activated at 0.5 C for ten cycles and then longer cycles. An ultrastable cycling stability was achieved for all three rates. As seen, the reversible capacity was 146 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C after 8000 cycles. The capacity retention was excellent even at higher current densities for long cycles. When cycled at 10 C, a high reversible capacity of 129 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was delivered in the 20 000 cycles. With a further increase in the current density to 20 C, the capacity was still as high as 116 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 10 000 cycles. The super-high rate performance and ultralong cycling stability at high charge/discharge rates were remarkably higher than those in previously reported studies on TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials for SIBs (as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0003">3</a>F and Table S1, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0014_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0016_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano">16</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0035_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0036_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0040_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0042_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">42</a>-<a href="#adma201705788-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0044_R_d227705608e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a></span> The superior high rate cycling ­stability is intimately connected with the excellent structural stability of the 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs. The post-mortem study (Figure S18, Supporting Information) showed that the structure of the 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs was well maintained after 800 cycles of discharging/charging at a current density of 0.5 C. To further study the phase change in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode, an ex situ XRD analysis was used to investigate the cycling in a half cell (Figure S19, Supporting Information). Clearly, for different voltages in the discharging/charging process, the XRD patterns were nearly the same except for slight changes in the position and a relative intensity of some of the peaks, indicating the good stability of the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs in half-cell cycling.</p>
         
         <p>To understand why 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs exhibited a better electrochemical performance compared to m-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was used to investigate the different charge/discharge levels. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>A shows the potential responses of both the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG and m-TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes, and the dotted lines represent the equilibrium open-circuit voltages (OCVs). The overpotentials for both electrodes were initially stable but slowly increased at the end of the discharging step. However, the overpotentials quickly increased during the entire charging step. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>B, the reaction resistances at different Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction levels were calculated by dividing the overpotential by the pulse current. During the discharging process, the resistances for both electrodes were stable at the platform and then increased to a high level. The charging process behavior was different, and relatively stable resistances were observed, except for a slight increase at the end of the process. The differences in the overpotentials and reaction resistances between the discharging and charging processes can be ­attributed to the different reaction mechanisms of the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction processes. Clearly, TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG exhibited a higher capacity, smaller polarization, and lower reaction resistance compared with that of m-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and this indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction kinetics on the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode are facilitated compared to those on the m-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. The inherent difference should originate from the smaller charge transfer resistance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode (Figure S20, Supporting Information), which was confirmed by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). Therefore, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode has improved reaction kinetics, which can result in rapid electron and ion transport during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertions/extractions and can lead to a remarkable enhancement in the rate performance. Additionally, the phase angle of the inclined line was larger than 45° for both samples (Figure S20, Supporting Information), indicating a significant capacitive component in sodium insertion/extraction,<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0045_R_d227705608e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Power Sources">45</a></span> which will be discussed in detail below.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201705788-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce13b2b9-8afd-49ea-9925-b36713a350ca/adma201705788-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce13b2b9-8afd-49ea-9925-b36713a350ca/adma201705788-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c7453e67-5fde-44bb-bf22-a26e44dd18c2/adma201705788-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce13b2b9-8afd-49ea-9925-b36713a350ca/adma201705788-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201705788-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201705788" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetics analysis and first-principle calculations of the sodium storage behavior for the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode. A) GITT voltage profiles. B) Reaction resistance. C) CV curves at different scan rates from 0.1 to 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. D) <i>b</i>-value analysis using the relationship between the peak currents and the scan rates. E) Normalized capacity versus sweep rate<sup>−1/2</sup>; inset: separation of the capacitive (shaded region) and diffusion currents in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG nanosheets at a scan rate of 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. F) Contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charges at different scan rates. Calculated DOSs of G) m-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H) TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG. I) Sodium diffusion pathway and corresponding energy profile from the electrolyte to the NFG and TiO<sub>2</sub> interface; Insets show the structure of the N-doped defect and sodium diffusion pathway.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To explore the reaction kinetics of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode, we further tested the CV curves at different sweep rates from 0.1 to 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>C, all the CV curves have a uniform shape with broad peaks for the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes. The small and nearly identical peak separations at scan rates below 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> resulted in a smaller polarization in the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs during both the cathodic and anodic processes and in a good rate performance (inset of Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>C and Figure S21, Supporting Information). The previously mentioned capacitive effect can be qualitatively analyzed using the power-law formula, <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, which is based on the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>) in the CV curves.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0046_R_d227705608e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Mater.">46</a></span> The <i>b</i>-value can be obtained from the slope of the log(<i>i</i>)–log(<i>v</i>) plots. Typically, <i>b</i> = 0.5 represents a diffusion-­controlled intercalation process, whereas <i>b</i> = 1.0 suggests a capacitive process. Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>D shows the log(<i>i</i>)–log(<i>v</i>) plots for the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode. For sweep rates lower than 20 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, <i>b</i> = 0.96 for the anodic peaks and 0.91 for the cathodic peaks, indicating that the fast kinetics were governed by a surface-induced capacitive process. For sweep rates above 20 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the slopes of the plots changed, and the <i>b</i>-value decreased to 0.65 for both the cathodic and anodic peaks. At a high scan rate, a capability limitation can arise from the solid–electrolyte interphase resistance, active material resistance, and diffusion constraints.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0046_R_d227705608e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Mater.">46</a></span> Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>E shows the normalized capacity versus <i>v</i><sup>−1/2</sup> plot for the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode. Clearly, the plot is divided into two regions. In region 1, for sweep rates &lt; 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity does not considerably change as the scan rate increases, which suggests that the capacitive contributions are not dominated by the sweep rate. In contrast, in region 2, for sweep rates &gt; 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity linearly decreases with <i>v</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, which indicates that the charge storage is mainly diffusion-controlled at high sweep rates. That is, for shorter charging times, the diffusion is rate-limiting, which is similar to most traditional battery electrodes. However, for longer charging times, a diffusion limitation does not occur, and the intercalation-based system behaves in a fully capacitive manner.</p>
         
         <p>The capacitive contribution at a given scan rate can be quantified by distinguishing it from the total charge storage, including the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charge. At a fixed potential, the contributions from the two separate mechanisms can be derived from the formula <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0047_R_d227705608e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">47</a>, <a href="#adma201705788-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0048_R_d227705608e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Phys. Chem. C">48</a></span> As shown in the inset of Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>E, 85.5% of the total charge is attributed to the capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The peak voltage generates the majority of the diffusion-controlled charge, which corresponds to the redox reaction between Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0048_R_d227705608e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Phys. Chem. C">48</a></span> The contribution ratios for the two different charge storage processes at other sweep rates can also be determined. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>F, the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode had a 71.1% capacitance contribution and 28.9% diffusion contribution at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As the sweep rate increased, the capacitance contribution gradually increases and reaches a maximum value of 95.5% at 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As a result, the high proportion of the capacitive contribution promotes the rate performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG electrode.</p>
         
         <p>As noted, when evaluating the influence of the NFG coating on the conductivity and kinetic properties of the TiO<sub>2</sub> host material, knowledge on the exact structure of the density of states (DOSs) is vital, and this was explored using the CP2K density functional theory (DFT) package. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>G, for the bare m-TiO<sub>2</sub>, the band gap between the valance band (VB) and the conduction band (CB) is ≈3 eV. In contrast, the coated NFG is a conductor and has contributions from bands near the Fermi level (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>H). Thus, electrons can easily transfer along the NFG. The N-doped defects on the graphene layers provide sodium channels from the electrolyte to the TiO<sub>2</sub> surface (Figure <a href="#adma201705788-fig-0004">4</a>I). The calculated energy barrier for sodium diffusion from the two NFG layers to the TiO<sub>2</sub> (101) surface is only 0.56 eV, which can be achieved at room temperature. This type of N-doped graphene defect leads to shorter diffusion paths for the ions and a larger electrolyte contact area.</p>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we synthesized TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs using dual-functional C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> sheets as both the sacrificial template and hybrid carbon source for the first time. In the obtained structure, interconnected, uniform TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were encapsulated in the few-layered graphene network and preferentially assembled into hierarchical, porous, 2D hybrid nanosheets. The graphene coating improved the conductivity and stability of the hybrid structure and restricted the size of the TiO<sub>2</sub> particles, which benefitted the sodium storage performance. The TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs showed a high reversible capacity, ultralong cycling stability, and exceptionally high rate performance, and they delivered a reversible capacity of 129 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C for over 20 000 cycles and 116 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C for over 10 000 cycles without detectable fading as well as an ultrahigh rate capability up to 60 C with a capacity of 101 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Considering the simple synthetic method to prepare the unique, 2D hybrid nanostructure on a large scale, TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG is promising as a high-power and high-energy anode material for SIBs. We conclude that the superior sodium storage properties originate from the unique, hierarchical, porous, 2D hybrid nanostructure, which allows for good electrolyte diffusion in the pores, good electronic conductivity via the interconnected network of ultrathin graphitic carbon shells, and fast sodium-ion reaction kinetics based on the results of the sodiation dynamics analysis and first-principle calculations.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201705788-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201705788-sec-0020-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets</i>: First, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanosheets were synthesized according to previous work.<span><a href="#adma201705788-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201705788-bib-0026_R_d227705608e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></span> Briefly, 15 g of urea was placed in a crucible (100 mL) with a lid and heated to 550 °C for 4 h in a muffle furnace at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. After the crucible was cooled to room temperature, the resulting yellow g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanosheets were collected for use. To prepare the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, 0.2 g of the freshly prepared g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanosheets was dispersed in 200 mL of ethanol under sonication for 1 h. Then, 0.9 mL of ammonia was dropped into the suspension under stirring for 0.5 h. After that, 1.5 mL of TBOT was injected into the above mixture. The as-obtained, light-yellow mixture was transferred to a 250 mL round-bottom flask, maintained at 45 °C for 20 h and then naturally cooled to room temperature. The resulting samples were collected and washed via rinsing and centrifugation several times with deionized (DI) water and absolute ethanol and then dried in an oven at 60 °C overnight prior to further characterization.</p>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs</i>: To prepare the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were calcined at 600 °C for 2 h at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> under a vacuum atmosphere. The synthesis procedure used for the m-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets was the same as that used for preparing the TiO<sub>2</sub>@NFG HPHNSs, and the only difference is that laboratory air instead of a vacuum was used during the heating step.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The crystallographic structures of the products were examined using powder XRD (Bruker D8 Advance, Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5418 Å). The dimensions, morphologies, and structures of the products were characterized by FESEM (GeminiSEM 500), TEM (JEOL, JEM-1011), HRTEM, EELS maps, and HAADF-STEM images (JEOL JEM-ARM 200F field-emission atomic-resolution analytical microscope). The texture properties of the samples were determined by measuring the nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms (an Autosorb 6B instrument) at 77 K. The surface compositions of the samples were investigated by XPS (Thermo ESCALAB 250Xi) using Al Kα (<i>hν</i>    = 1486.6 eV) X-ray radiation, and the binding energies were calibrated using the C 1s peak (284.8 eV) as a reference. The TGA of the samples was performed on a Mettler Toledo TGA/SDTA851 thermal analyzer. Raman spectra were obtained with a LabRAM HR800 spectrometer.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The electrochemical tests were conducted using two-electrode coin cells (size: 2032) with pure sodium foil serving as both the counter electrode and the reference electrode. The working electrode, including 70 wt% active material, 20 wt% conductivity agent (acetylene black, Super P), and 10 wt% binder (carboxy methyl cellulose, CMC), was ground into a homogeneous slurry, coated onto a commercial copper foil (Shenzhen BiYuan Electronics) with a thickness of 200 µm and dried at 60 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h. After that, the electrode was cut into disks with diameters of 12 mm. The loading of the active material was ≈1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The cell electrolyte contained 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethylene carbonate (DEC) with 5 vol% of fluoroethylene carbonate. All the assembly processes were performed in an argon-filled glovebox with both moisture and oxygen contents below 1 ppm. The galvanostatic cycle measurements were conducted on battery testers (LAND-CT2001A) with voltage windows of 0.01–3.0 V. The CV tests were performed using an electrochemical workstation (CHI760D) in the voltage range 0.01–3.0 V. EIS was performed on an electrochemical workstation (CHI760D) in a frequency range of 0.1 MHz to 0.01 Hz and the measured potential is 0.85 V in charging process. The GITT measurements were conducted using a current density of 0.1 C as the pulse and lasted for equal durations of 20 min. After each pulse, the cells rested with an open circuit for 2 h to reach the equilibrium potential.</p>
         
         <p><i>DFT Calculations</i>: All DFT calculations were performed using the CP2K simulation package. The DFT +<i>U</i> method with <i>U</i> = 5 eV was used to describe the localized Ti 3d states. The spin-polarized PBE exchange–correlation functional was adopted with the double-ζ Gaussian (DZVP) basis sets, which were used for the valence electrons. An auxiliary plane-wave basis set with a cutoff of 350 Ry was used to compute the electrostatic terms. A gamma point approximation was used for the Brillouin zone integration. The transition state and sodium diffusion pathway calculations were performed using the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method. An anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (101)-p(3 × 4) 18-layer supercell with the bottom six layers fixed as the surface slab model was used to model the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. In addition, two layers of N-doped graphene were used to model the NFG coating on the TiO<sub>2</sub> (101) surface.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
